        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acsami.1c15356"><meta property="og:title" content="[Co3(μ3-O)]-Based Metal–Organic Frameworks as Advanced Anode Materials in K- and Na-Ion Batteries"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c15356/asset/images/acsami.1c15356.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="A new metal–organic framework {(Me2NH2)2[Co3(μ3-O)(btb)2(py)(H2O)]·(DMF)2(H2O)2}n (Cobtbpy) was solvothermal synthesized (H3btb = 1,3,5-tri(4-carboxylphenyl)benzene, py = pyridine, DMF = N,N-dimethylformamide). Cobtbpy shows a (3,6)-connected rtl 3D network with a point symbol of (4·62)2(42·610·83) based on the [Co3(μ3-O)] clusters. The obtained Cobtbpy has stable, accessible, dense active sites and can be applied in the potassium- and sodium-ion batteries. Through mixing with single-walled carbon nanotubes, the prepared composite anode material Cobtbpy-0.9 achieved a high reversible capability, delivering 416 mAh/g in the potassium-ion batteries and 379 mAh/g in the sodium-ion batteries at 0.05 A/g. The outstanding properties of Cobtbpy-0.9 in the batteries demonstrated that this MOFs-based carbon composite is a highly desirable electrode material candidate for high-performance potassium- and sodium-ion batteries."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c15356/asset/images/acsami.1c15356.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/nwyw~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="90da88518ac8b325-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da88518ac8b325-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: '48cb21e3-cf78-49be-beb9-75336bb8521a'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

type="text/x-mathjax-config;executed=true">MathJax.Hub.Config({
            menuSettings: {
                zoom: "Click",
                zscale: "150%"
            },
            SVG: {
                fonts: ["STIX-Web"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
            },
            "HTML-CSS": {
                fonts: ["STIX"],
                linebreaks: { automatic: true },
                scale: 90,
            },
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("mml Jax Ready", function () {
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            MML.mi.prototype.setTeXclass = function (prev) {
                this.getPrevClass(prev);
                return this;
            };
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                setTeXclass: function (prev) {
                    this.getPrevClass(prev);
                    for (var i = 0, m = this.data.length; i < m; i++) {
                        if (this.data[i]) this.data[i].setTeXclass();
                    }
                    return this;
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("SVG Jax Ready", function () {
            var FONT = MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"] || MathJax.OutputJax.SVG.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"];
            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("MathML Jax Ready", function () {
            var PARSE = MathJax.InputJax.MathML.Parse;
            var ADD = PARSE.prototype.AddChildren;
            PARSE.Augment({
                AddChildren: function (mml, node) {
                    ADD.call(this, mml, node);
                    if (mml.type === 'mrow') {
                        var first = mml.data[0], last = mml.data[mml.data.length-1];
                        if (mml.open && !mml.data[0].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.open;
                        if (mml.close && !mml.data[mml.data.length - 1].Get('stretchy')) delete mml.close;
                    }
                }
            });
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS Jax Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax['HTML-CSS'];
            var DELIMITERS = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.DELIMITERS;
            var FONT = HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-variant"] || HTMLCSS.FONTDATA.VARIANT["-STIX-Web-variant"]

            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;

            MML.mrow.Augment({
              _HTMLlineBreaks: MML.mrow.prototype.HTMLlineBreaks,
              HTMLlineBreaks: function (span) {

                if (this.parent.linebreakContainer && this.Get("display") === "inline") return false;
                return this._HTMLlineBreaks(span);
              }
            });


            DELIMITERS[0x0305] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x00AF};
            DELIMITERS[0x033F] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x003D};
            DELIMITERS[0x20E1] = {dir: "H", alias: 0x2194};

            if (FONT) {
                FONT.remap[0xB0]= [0x25E6, "normal"];
                FONT.remap[0x2A]= [0x2217, "normal"];
            }

            delete MathJax.ElementJax.mml.mo.prototype.remapChars['°'];
        });

        MathJax.Hub.Register.StartupHook("HTML-CSS multiline Ready", function () {
            var HTMLCSS = MathJax.OutputJax["HTML-CSS"];
            var MML = MathJax.ElementJax.mml;
            var PENALTY = MML.mbase.prototype.HTMLlinebreakPenalty;

            MML.msqrt.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mroot.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mfrac.Augment({
                linebreakContainer: false,
                HTMLbetterBreak: function () {return false}
            });
            MML.mspace.Augment({
                defaultDef: {
                    indentalign: MML.INDENTALIGN.AUTO,
                    indentshift: "0",
                    indenttarget: "",
                    indentalignfirst: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftfirst: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT,
                    indentalignlast: MML.INDENTALIGN.INDENTALIGN,
                    indentshiftlast: MML.INDENTSHIFT.INDENTSHIFT
                },
                HTMLbetterBreak: function (info,state) {
                    if (info.values && info.values.id === this.spanID) {return false}
                    var values = this.getValues(
                        "linebreak",
                        "indentalign","indentshift",
                        "indentalignfirst","indentshiftfirst",
                        "indentalignlast","indentshiftlast"
                    );
                    var linebreakValue = values.linebreak;
                    if (!linebreakValue || this.hasDimAttr()) {
                        linebreakValue = MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO;
                    }
                    var W = info.scanW, span = this.HTMLspanElement(), w = span.bbox.w;
                    if (span.style.paddingLeft) {w += HTMLCSS.unEm(span.style.paddingLeft)}
                    if (W - info.shift === 0) {return false} // don't break at zero width
                    var offset = HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth - W;

                    if (state.n === 0 && (values.indentshiftfirst !== state.VALUES.indentshiftfirst ||
                        values.indentalignfirst !== state.VALUES.indentalignfirst)) {
                        var align = this.HTMLgetAlign(state,values),
                            shift = this.HTMLgetShift(state,values,align);
                        offset += (info.shift - shift);
                    }

                    var penalty = Math.floor(offset / HTMLCSS.linebreakWidth * 1000);
                    if (penalty < 0) {penalty = PENALTY.toobig - 3*penalty}
                    penalty += info.nest * PENALTY.nestfactor;

                    var linebreak = PENALTY[linebreakValue]||0;
                    if (linebreakValue === MML.LINEBREAK.AUTO && w >= PENALTY.spacelimit &&
                        !this.mathbackground && !this.background)
                    {linebreak = [(w+PENALTY.spaceoffset)*PENALTY.spacefactor]}
                    if (!MathJax.Object.isArray(linebreak)) {
                        if (linebreak || offset >= 0) {penalty = linebreak * info.nest}
                    } else {penalty = Math.max(1,penalty + linebreak[0] * info.nest)}

                    if (penalty >= info.penalty) {return false}
                    info.penalty = penalty; info.values = values; info.W = W; info.w = w;
                    values.lineleading = state.VALUES.lineleading;
                    values.linebreakstyle = "before"; values.id = this.spanID;
                    return true;
                }
            });


            
            PENALTY.goodbreak = [-400];

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="90da88518ac8b325-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'applied_materials_interfaces';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/applied_materials_interfaces/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

<script nonce="90da88518ac8b325-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "aamick";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "[Co&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;(μ&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;-O)]-Based Metal–Organic Frameworks as Advanced Anode Materials in K- and Na-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c15356", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "[Co&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;(μ&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;-O)]-Based Metal–Organic Frameworks as Advanced Anode Materials in K- and Na-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c15356", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "[Co&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;(μ&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;-O)]-Based Metal–Organic Frameworks as Advanced Anode Materials in K- and Na-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c15356", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "[Co&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;(μ&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;-O)]-Based Metal–Organic Frameworks as Advanced Anode Materials in K- and Na-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c15356", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "[Co&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;(μ&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;-O)]-Based Metal–Organic Frameworks as Advanced Anode Materials in K- and Na-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c15356", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "[Co&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;(μ&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;-O)]-Based Metal–Organic Frameworks as Advanced Anode Materials in K- and Na-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c15356", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "[Co&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;(μ&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;-O)]-Based Metal–Organic Frameworks as Advanced Anode Materials in K- and Na-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c15356", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "[Co&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;(μ&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;-O)]-Based Metal–Organic Frameworks as Advanced Anode Materials in K- and Na-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c15356", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "[Co&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;(μ&lt;sub&gt;3&lt;/sub&gt;-O)]-Based Metal–Organic Frameworks as Advanced Anode Materials in K- and Na-Ion Batteries", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acsami.1c15356", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

Society</div><div class="article_rightPermissionsLink"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone4" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone4"><div class="articleRightsPermissionsLink"><a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D46902%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DGuo-Ping%2BYang%252C%2BXiao-Xi%2BLuo%252C%2BYu-Feng%2BLiu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D39%26contentID%3Dacsami.1c15356%26title%3D%255BCo3%2528%25CE%25BC3-O%2529%255D-Based%2BMetal%25E2%2580%2593Organic%2BFrameworks%2Bas%2BAdvanced%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bin%2BK-%2Band%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D46908%26publicationDate%3DOctober%2B2021" Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c15356/asset/images/medium/am1c15356_0005.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c15356/asset/images/large/am1c15356_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c15356&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">A new metal–organic framework {(Me<sub>2</sub>NH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub>[Co<sub>3</sub>(μ<sub>3</sub>-O)(btb)<sub>2</sub>(py)(H<sub>2</sub>O)]·(DMF)<sub>2</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>2</sub>}<sub>n</sub> (<b>Cobtbpy</b>) was solvothermal synthesized (H<sub>3</sub>btb = 1,3,5-tri(4-carboxylphenyl)benzene, py = pyridine, DMF = N,N-dimethylformamide). <b>Cobtbpy</b> shows a (3,6)-connected <b>rtl</b> 3D network with a point symbol of (4·6<sup>2</sup>)<sub>2</sub>(4<sup>2</sup>·6<sup>10</sup>·8<sup>3</sup>) based on the [Co<sub>3</sub>(μ<sub>3</sub>-O)] clusters. The obtained <b>Cobtbpy</b> has stable, accessible, dense active sites and can be applied in the potassium- and sodium-ion batteries. Through mixing with single-walled carbon nanotubes, the prepared composite anode material <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 achieved a high reversible capability, delivering 416 mAh/g in the potassium-ion batteries and 379 mAh/g in the sodium-ion batteries at 0.05 A/g. The outstanding properties of <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 in the batteries demonstrated that this MOFs-based carbon composite is a highly desirable electrode material candidate for high-performance potassium- and sodium-ion batteries.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D46902%26pageCount%3D7%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DGuo-Ping%2BYang%252C%2BXiao-Xi%2BLuo%252C%2BYu-Feng%2BLiu%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D39%26contentID%3Dacsami.1c15356%26title%3D%255BCo3%2528%25CE%25BC3-O%2529%255D-Based%2BMetal%25E2%2580%2593Organic%2BFrameworks%2Bas%2BAdvanced%2BAnode%2BMaterials%2Bin%2BK-%2Band%2BNa-Ion%2BBatteries%26numPages%3D7%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D46908%26publicationDate%3DOctober%2B2021" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291517&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Batteries" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Batteries" class="article__tags__link">Batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291646&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Carbon nanotubes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Carbon%20nanotubes" class="article__tags__link">Carbon nanotubes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Composites" class="article__tags__link">Composites</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Metal%20organic%20frameworks" class="article__tags__link">Metal organic frameworks</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Metal%E2%88%92organic+Framework&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_metal%E2%88%92organic%20framework">metal−organic framework</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Battery&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_battery">battery</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Anode+Materials&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_anode%20materials">anode materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The safety issue of lithium-ion batteries is still a severe problem as well as other manufacturing issues even though they have been widely used in commercial applications. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3 ref4" aria-label="reference 1 to 4">(1−4)</a> There is a popular belief that the development of K- and Na-ion batteries is a potential plan b for commercial energy storage application because K and Na elements are rich and easily accessible on the earth. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8 ref9" aria-label="reference 5 to 9">(5−9)</a> K<sup>+</sup>/K and Na<sup>+</sup>/Na show relatively low potential, which are close to Li<sup>+</sup>/Li. The development of K- and Na-ion batteries make it possible to obtain higher energy densities. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 10 to 14">(10−14)</a> However, the exploration of K- and Na-ion batteries are still in the infant stages because few materials can tolerate the repeated migration of the large-sized K<sup>+</sup> or Na<sup>+</sup> as the anode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19 ref20" aria-label="reference 15 to 20">(15−20)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">To solve the problems mentioned above, a number of anode materials are being studied for K- and Na-ion batteries, such as Se or Sb based material and carbon composite, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 21 to 27">(21−27)</a> and it is still of great value to explore K- and Na-ion batteries systems. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28 ref29 ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 28 to 32">(28−32)</a> The well-known metal–organic frameworks (MOFs) represent a kind of crystalline solid materials with many characteristics such as multiple and devisible structures, high specific surface area and porosity, tunable pores, as well as other controllable chemical and physical properties. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref34 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 33 to 35">(33−35)</a> These multitude of advantages make MOFs promising prospects in various applications including gas adsorption and separation, sensors, energy storage, catalysis, drug delivery, and so on. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36 ref37 ref38 ref39 ref40" aria-label="reference 36 to 40">(36−40)</a> It is worth noting that because of the tunable supramolecular components, MOFs are particularly suitable for electrochemical applications. MOFs with robust framework and wide variety of pores could store K<sup>+</sup> or Na<sup>+</sup> very well as a potential electrode material in K- and Na-ion batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46 ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46 ref47" aria-label="reference 41 to 47">(41−47)</a> MOFs acting as anode materials in batteries have drawn lots of attention in recent years. However, MOFs have a poor electrical conductivity, which is harmful for electron transportation. Great efforts have been made to improve the electron conductivity of MOFs because pure MOFs anode materials are usually limited by poor electron conductivity, reversible capacities, and rate performances. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref38 ref39 ref48 ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38 ref39 ref48 ref49" aria-label="reference 37 to 39, 48 and 49">(37−39,48,49)</a> Multifarious MOFs-based composite materials were explored in order to overcome the disadvantages of MOFs-based electrodes including carbon composite, metallic oxides, carbides, chalcogenides, phosphides, etc. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50 ref51 ref52 ref53 ref54'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50 ref51 ref52 ref53 ref54" aria-label="reference 50 to 54">(50−54)</a> What is noteworthy is that mixing MOFs with single-walled carbon nanotubes (SWCNTs) is an efficient method to improve the performance in battery tests. The introduction of SWCNTs could solve the low conductivity of MOFs, and the construction of the cross-linked carbon network also shorten the ion diffusion pathways, which contributes to take full advantages of electroactive sites in MOFs.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this work, a new MOFs {(Me<sub>2</sub>NH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub>[Co<sub>3</sub>(μ<sub>3</sub>-O)(btb)<sub>2</sub>(py)(H<sub>2</sub>O)]·(DMF)<sub>2</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>2</sub>}<sub>n</sub> (<b>Cobtbpy</b>) with 3D anionic <b>rtl</b> frameworks was synthesized (H<sub>3</sub>btb = 1,3,5-tri(4-carboxylphenyl)benzene, py = pyridine, DMF = <i>N</i>,<i>N</i>-dimethylformamide). Through simple ball mixing, <b>Cobtbpy</b>/SWCNTs composite material (<b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9) was prepared and exhibited excellent electrochemical performances in K- and Na-ion batteries as the anode.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Structure of Cobtbpy</h3><div class="NLM_p"><b>Cobtbpy</b> crystallizes in the space group of <i>Pnma</i> and shows a (3,6)-connected 3D anionic metal–organic frameworks with 2-fold interpenetration based on [Co<sub>3</sub>(μ<sub>3</sub>-O)] clusters. In the [Co<sub>3</sub>(μ<sub>3</sub>-O)] moiety, Co1 is coordinated by four carboxylate oxygen atoms (O1, O1A, O3C, O3D) from four btb ligands, one μ<sub>3</sub>-O (O7) and one nitrogen atom (N1) from one pyridine in a slightly distorted octahedral geometry with a CoO<sub>5</sub>N chromophore. Co2 is also coordinated by four carboxylate oxygen atoms (O2, O4B, O5D, O6D) from three btb ligands, one μ<sub>3</sub>-O (O7) and one water molecule (O8) in a distorted octahedral geometry. Co1, O7, and the coordinated pyridine locate on the symmetry plane of a equicrural triangle. The μ<sub>3</sub>-O (O7) connects three Co(II) ions (Co1, Co2, and Co2A) and forms a trinuclear Co(II) cluster [Co<sub>3</sub>(μ<sub>3</sub>-O)] almost in a plane. O7 is 0.081(4) Å away from the Co(II) plane constructed by Co1, Co2, and Co2A. The bond angles of Co1–O7–Co2, Co1–O7–Co2A, and Co2–O7–Co2A are 113.933(15)°, 113.933(15)°, and 131.656(20)°, respectively. The distances of Co(II) atoms in [Co<sub>3</sub>(μ<sub>3</sub>-O)] cluster are 3.4916(6) Å for Co1···Co2 or Co1···Co2A and 3.6906(10) Å for Co2···Co2A (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a).</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c15356/asset/images/medium/am1c15356_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c15356/asset/images/large/am1c15356_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. (a) Coordinating environment of <b>Cobtbpy</b>; (b) coordination mode of btb ligand in <b>Cobtbpy</b>; (c) the 3D packing structure of <b>Cobtbpy</b> in space-filling mode with 2-fold interpenetration; (d) topological schematic depiction of the 2-fold interpenetrated (3,6)-connected 3D network,pink and blue balls represent btb ligands and [Co<sub>3</sub>(μ<sub>3</sub>-O)] clusters, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c15356/asset/images/large/am1c15356_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c15356&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The carboxylates of btb ligand in <b>Cobtbpy</b> show two kinds of coordinating modes. Two carboxylate groups (O1/O2 and O3/O4) of btb have a μ<sub>2</sub>-η<sup>1</sup>: η<sup>1</sup> bidentate mode and connect one Co1 and one Co2. The other carboxylate group (O5/O6) shows a monodentate chelating mode with one Co2 (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b). One [Co<sub>3</sub>(μ<sub>3</sub>-O)] cluster is coordinated by six btb ligands, one pyridine and two water molecules. Thus, the 2-fold interpenetrated 3D anionic framework of <b>Cobtbpy</b> is constrcuted by the connection of [Co<sub>3</sub>(μ<sub>3</sub>-O)] clusters and btb ligands (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c). The holes and channels in the anionic frameworks are occupied by disordered dimethylamine cations, dimethylformamide and water molecules with the solvent accessible volume of 4519 Å<sup>3</sup>, which is 51.5% of the cell volume calculated by <i>PLATON</i>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55 ref56 ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55 ref56 ref57" aria-label="reference 55 to 57">(55−57)</a> The irregular rhomboid channels in <b>Cobtbpy</b> along the <i>a</i>-axis have 1.7 Å minimum width and 6.7 Å maximum width (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58" aria-label="reference 58">(58)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Topologically, each btb ligand connects three [Co<sub>3</sub>(μ<sub>3</sub>-O)] clusters and should be considered as a 3-connected node with a point symbol of (4·6<sup>2</sup>)<sub>2</sub>. One [Co<sub>3</sub>(μ<sub>3</sub>-O)] cluster is coordinated by six btb ligands and can be treated as a 6-connected node with a point symbol of (4<sup>2</sup>·6<sup>10</sup>·8<sup>3</sup>) (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="Supporting Information (SI) Figure S1a">Supporting Information (SI) Figure S1a</a>). The 3D architecture of <b>Cobtbpy</b> shows a binodal (3,6)-connected <b>rtl</b> 3D topological network with a point symbol of (4·6<sup>2</sup>)<sub>2</sub>(4<sup>2</sup>·6<sup>10</sup>·8<sup>3</sup>) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d and <a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="SI Figure S1b">SI Figure S1b</a>).</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Electrochemical Performance</h3><div class="NLM_p">The bulk <b>Cobtbpy</b> samples without stripping is in micrometer-sized particles (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="SI Figure S2a">SI Figure S2a</a>). However, the large-size particle would be adverse for the diffusion of the K<sup>+</sup> or Na<sup>+</sup> in electrochemical reactions. In order to reduce the size and improve the electric conductivity of <b>Cobtbpy</b>, SWCNTs is added and stripped by balling. On the one hand, the particles sizes of <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 become smaller obviously which is beneficial for shortening the ion transport pathways. On the other hand, the bulk particle strips into layers and SWCNTs inset between the layers successfully, which could connect <b>Cobtbpy</b> layers effectively and form cross-linked conductive networks (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="SI Figure S2b">SI Figure S2b</a>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a></div><div class="NLM_p"><b>Cobtbpy</b> and <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 are tested in K-ion batteries with metallic K as counter electrode from 0.01 to 3 V (vs K<sup>+</sup>/K). The reversible capacityof <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 is up to 416 mAh/g at 0.05 A/g, which is higher than <b>Cobtbpy</b>. Irreversible decomposition of electrolyte, the formation of solid-state interphase (SEI) membrane, and partial K-ion trapping in the electroactive sites may be the reasons of the capacity loss of few first cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a,c). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11 ref12 ref15 ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11 ref12 ref15 ref16" aria-label="reference 11, 12, 15 and 16">(11,12,15,16)</a> Compared with <b>Cobtbpy</b>, the rate performance improve a lot. By stripping to the thinner layers, not only more electroactive sites could be exposed but also shortening the ion transportation pathways. The capacity of <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 is higher than <b>Cobtbpy</b> at same current density (361, 312, 282, 247, 226, 200 mAh/g for 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.5, 0.7, 1 A/g, respectively) and with the increase of current, the structure of the electrode material remains stable, which is convenient for the de/intercalation of K<sup>+</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b,c). The capacityof <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 electrode is still up to 377 mAh/g when the current density returns to 0.05 A/g (89.5% capacity retention). In addition, <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 exhibits a perfect stability at 0.1 A/g (activating in five cycles at 0.05 A/g before the formal cycles). With the addition of SWCNTs, the composite anode does not appear obvious decay. The capacityof <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 is still up to 321 mAh/g (85% retention) after 85 cycles and Coulombic efficiency is nearly close to 100% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d). However, <b>Cobtbpy</b> electrode only has 202 mAh/g (66% retention). The good stability of <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 may contributes to the electron transportation. Meanwhile, the distances between layers enlarge through balling to enable the K<sup>+</sup> de/intercalation.</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c15356/asset/images/medium/am1c15356_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c15356/asset/images/large/am1c15356_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Electrochemical performance of <b>Cobtbpy</b> and <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 in K-ion batteries. (a) galvanostatic charge–discharge (GCD) curves for the first to third cycles at 0.05 A/g in K-ion batteries, respectively; (b) GCD curves under different current densities; (c) rate capabilities under different current densities; (d) the cycling performance at 0.1 A/g.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c15356/asset/images/large/am1c15356_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c15356&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The Na-ion batteries performances of <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 are also studied using half-cell system from 0.01 to 3 V (vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). It is found that the charge/discharge curves at 0.05 A/g display a similar activation process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a). Irreversible decomposition of electrolyte, the formation of SEI and the stripping of Na-ion may be the reasons of the capacity loss of first cycle. The reversible capacity of <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 is up to 379 mAh/g. As the current density increases, the <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 still has 292, 238, 203, 151, 135, 114 mAh/g at 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.5, 0.7, 1 A/g, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b,c). Although the capacity of <b>Cobtbpy</b> is higher, it is unstable and has significant decay. Otherwise, <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 also has a good cycle stability, after activating in 5 cycles at 0.05 A/g. The charge capacity of <b>Cobtbpy</b> is 302 mAh/g, but only 221 mAh/g remained after 50 cycles. However, <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 shows a more superior cycle performance. It has 348 mAh/g at first and the electrode still has 306 mAh/g after 50 cycles (88% retention), and the Coulombic efficiency is 97%, almost 100% (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c15356/asset/images/medium/am1c15356_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c15356/asset/images/large/am1c15356_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. Electrochemical performance of <b>Cobtbpy</b> and <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 in Na-ion batteries. (a) GCD curves for the first to third cycles under 0.05 A/g in Na-ion batteries, respectively; (b) GCD curves under different current densities; (c) rate capabilities under different current densities; (d) the cycling performance at 0.1 A/g.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c15356/asset/images/large/am1c15356_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c15356&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical kinetics of <b>Cobtbpy-</b>0.9 are exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a–f and a–c for K-ion battery, d–f for Na-ion battery. The cyclic voltammogram (CV) curves of <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 electrode are obtained under the scan rate of 0.1 mV/s (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a). The reaction of generating solid electrolyte interphase peaks and the activation of the electrode during the first cathodic scan may cause the formation of the peak at 1.0 V. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref59'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref59" aria-label="reference 59">(59)</a> While K-ion may be the reason for the peak at 0.60 V. An obvious peak at 0.47 V is associated with K-ion deintercalation for the anodic scan. And it is worth mentioning that the cathodic peak belongs shift (0.60–0.77 V) is resulted from the formation of irreversible SEI membrane in the first cathodic cycle, which matches with GCD curves (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a). The consistency of anodic and cathodic peaks in the subsequent cycles indicates the stability and outstanding electrochemical reversibility of the <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 composite anode.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c15356/asset/images/medium/am1c15356_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c15356/asset/images/large/am1c15356_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) cyclic voltammograms of <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 at 0.1 mV/s in K-ion batteries; (b) cyclic voltammograms of <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 under different sweep rates in K-ion batteries; (c) <i>b</i>-values in equation under redox reaction peaks in K-ion batteries. (d) cyclic voltammograms of <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 at 0.1 mV/s in Na-ion batteries; (e) cyclic voltammograms of <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 under different sweep rates in Na-ion batteries; (f) <i>b</i>-values in equation under redox reaction peaks in Na-ion batteries.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c15356/asset/images/large/am1c15356_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.1c15356&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">To deeply understand the K-ion storage kinetics of the <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 composite anode, multiple scan rate curves (0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.5, 1.0 mV/s) are collected in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b. With the scan rate increasing, the curve shapes have almost no change, suggesting that the redox reaction is reversible without the infuence of scan rate. All curves exhibit one pair of redox peaks (named as peak I and II). The determination of b value in the equation as follows can reflect the K-ion storage kinetics,<span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.1c15356/asset/images/medium/am1c15356_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i6"></div><div class="label"></div></div></span>where <i>i</i> refers to current density and <i>v</i> represents scan rate, <i>b</i> is constant. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref60'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref60" aria-label="reference 19 and 60">(19,60)</a> It is acknowledged that when <i>b</i>-value is 0.5, it is a diffusion-controlled process, whereas 1.0 indicates a surfaced-controlled process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19 ref61 ref62'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19 ref61 ref62" aria-label="reference 19, 61 and 62">(19,61,62)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The <i>b</i>-values of peak I and II are calculated as 0.70 and 0.67 in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c, respectively, which indicates that the K-ion storage of the <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 anode is synergistically cocontrolled by the two mentioned mechanisms, and the surfaced-controlled process was dominant.</div><div class="NLM_p last">We also further study the electrochemical kinetics <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 for Na-ion batteries. The peak at 0.60 V should be triggered by the reaction of generating solid electrolyte interphase in during the first cathodic scan at 0.1 mV/s (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> And the subsequent cycles overlapped very well. A pair of cathodic/anodic peaks at 0.28/0.85 V (named as peak III and IV) and is observed obviously, which corresponds to the reaction of Na-ions with <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9. And these results are consistent with GCD curves in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a. To understand the storage process of Na-ion in <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9, cyclic voltammetry (CV) is carried out at sweep rates ranging from 0.1–0.3 mV/s (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>e). According to the equation, the <i>b</i>-values for peaks III and IV are 0.22 and 0.56, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>f). It is revealed that the Na-ion storage in <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 is controlled by solid-state-diffusion, which is different in the K-ion batteries.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In summary, we successfully solvothermal synthesized a new MOFs <b>(Cobtbpy</b>)with a novel (3,6)-connected 3D anionic <b>rtl</b> network based on the [Co<sub>3</sub>(μ<sub>3</sub>-O)] clusters. A MOFs-based carbon composite material (<b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9) was prepared by mixing <b>Cobtbpy</b> and single-walled carbon nanotubes. High density of redox-active sites, specific porosity, favorable conductivity, promote the <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 anode exhibited a remarkable rate capacity. The reversible capacities of <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 are up to 416 mAh/g in K-ion batteries and 379 mAh/g in Na-ion batteries at 0.05 A/g. The excellent chemical/thermal stability of <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 suggests a stable cycling life with a robust structure. All results demonstrated that <b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 is a desirable plan b for high-performance electrode materials in K- and Na-ion batteries.</div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i8">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec4_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Synthesis of {(Me<sub>2</sub>NH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub>[Co<sub>3</sub>(μ<sub>3</sub>-O)(btb)<sub>2</sub>(py)(H<sub>2</sub>O)]·(DMF)<sub>2</sub>(H<sub>2</sub>O)<sub>2</sub>}<sub>n</sub> (Cobtbpy)</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Co(OAc)<sub>2</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O (0.14 mmol, 0.0349 g), H<sub>3</sub>btb (0.26 mmol, 0.1140 g) and pyridine (0.31 mmol) were dissolved in a mixed solution of 1.5 mL C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>OH, 1.5 mL DMF and 1 mL H<sub>2</sub>O. The mixture was added into a Teflon-lined stainless-steel autoclave and heated at 90 °C. Dark violet block crystals were collected after 3 days (0.0409 g, 61% based on Co(OAc)<sub>2</sub>·4H<sub>2</sub>O). Anal. Calcd (%) for C<sub>69</sub>H<sub>71</sub>Co<sub>3</sub>N<sub>5</sub>O<sub>18</sub> (<b>Cobtbpy</b>): C, 57.75; H, 4.99; N, 4.88; found: C, 56.05; H, 4.74; N, 3.27. IR (cm<sup>–1</sup>): 3208 (w), 3060 (w), 1655 (m), 1583 (s), 1534 (m), 1386 (vs), 1252 (m), 1181 (w), 1103 (w), 1015 (w), 855 (m), 806 (w), 780 (vs), 701 (s), 667 (m), 636 (w).</div></div><div id="sec4_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Synthesis of the MOFs-Based Carbon Composite (Cobtbpy-0.9)</h3><div class="NLM_p last">PXRD pattern confirmed the purity of <b>Cobtbpy</b> sample (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#notes1" aria-label="SI Figure S3">SI Figure S3</a>). The obtained <b>Cobtbpy</b> sample was heated at 200 °C for 2 h in Ar atmosphere to remove guest molecules. Then <b>Cobtbpy</b> powder sample and SWCNTs are mixed in 9:1 ratio and ball milled for 1 h to prepare the composite material (<b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9).</div></div><div id="sec4_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i11" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i11"> Preparation of the Working Electrodes</h3><div class="NLM_p last"><b>Cobtbpy</b>-0.9 powders, carbon black conductive additive, and sodium carboxymethylcellulose binder were mixed inthe mass ratio of 6:3:1. Subsequently, deionized water was added to mixture and grinded for 30 min. The resulting slurry was coated on Cu foil and dried at 60 °C for overnight under vacuum. Each Cu foil afforded a mass of 1–1.5 mg/cm<sup>2</sup> for typical electrode. Glass microfiber filters were used as the separator. 0.8 M KPF<sub>6</sub> in ethylene carbonate/diethyl carbonate (v/v = 1:1), 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in propylene carbonate and 5% fluoroethylene carbonate were used as the electrolytes. K metal and Na metal were used as the anodes for the K- and Na-ion cells, respectively.</div></div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i12">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button                         cas_cit2R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">2</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Three-dimensional bilayer garnet solid electrolyte based high energy density lithium metal-sulfur batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Fu, Kun; Gong, Yunhui; Hitz, Gregory T.; McOwen, Dennis W.; Li, Yiju; Xu, Shaomao; Wen, Yang; Zhang, Lei; Wang, Chengwei; Pastel, Glenn; Dai, Jiaqi; Liu, Boyang; Xie, Hua; Yao, Yonggang; Wachsman, Eric D.; Hu, Liangbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Energy &amp; Environmental Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">7</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1568-1575</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">EESNBY</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1754-5706</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">To simultaneously address the challenges of chem./phys. short circuits and electrode vol. variation, we demonstrate a three-dimensional (3D) bilayer garnet solid-state electrolyte framework for advanced Li metal batteries.  The dense layer is reduced in thickness to a few microns and still retains good mech. stability, thereby enabling the safe use of Li metal anodes.  The thick porous layer acts as a mech. support for the thin dense layer which serves as a host for high loading of cathode materials and provides pathways for continuous ion transport.  Results show that the integrated sulfur cathode loading can reach &gt;7 mg cm-2 while the proposed hybrid Li-S battery exhibits a high initial coulombic efficiency (&gt;99.8%) and high av. coulombic efficiency (&gt;99%) during the subsequent cycles.  This electrolyte framework represents a promising strategy to revolutionize Li-metal batteries by transitioning to all-solid-state batteries and can be extended to other cathode materials.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoJMilwQPMU57Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liRjsYPPSdSBA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXns1Sqsrc%253D&amp;md5=49bc17bf068c4944823c3d40c0eed4c7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref3"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref3'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">3</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit3" data-doi="10.1038/nmat4821"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Han, X.                         cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Negating interfacial impedance in garnet-based solid-state Li metal batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Han, Xiaogang; Gong, Yunhui; Fu, Kun; He, Xingfeng; Hitz, Gregory T.; Dai, Jiaqi; Pearse, Alex; Liu, Boyang; Wang, Howard; Rubloff, Gary; Mo, Yifei; Thangadurai, Venkataraman; Wachsman, Eric D.; Hu, Liangbing</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">16</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">5</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">572-579</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NMAACR</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1476-1122</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Garnet-type solid-state electrolytes have attracted extensive attention due to their high ionic cond., approaching 1 mS cm-1, excellent environmental stability, and wide electrochem. stability window, from lithium metal to ∼6 V.  However, to date, there has been little success in the development of high-performance solid-state batteries using these exceptional materials, the major challenge being the high solid-solid interfacial impedance between the garnet electrolyte and electrode materials.  In this work, we effectively address the large interfacial impedance between a lithium metal anode and the garnet electrolyte using ultrathin aluminum oxide (Al2O3) by at. layer deposition.  Li7La2.75Ca0.25Zr1.75Nb0.25O12 (LLCZN) is the garnet compn. of choice in this work due to its reduced sintering temp. and increased lithium ion cond.  A significant decrease of interfacial impedance, from 1,710 Ω cm2 to 1 Ω cm2, was obsd. at room temp., effectively negating the lithium metal/garnet interfacial impedance.  Exptl. and computational results reveal that the oxide coating enables wetting of metallic lithium in contact with the garnet electrolyte surface and the lithiated-alumina interface allows effective lithium ion transport between the lithium metal anode and garnet electrolyte.  We also demonstrate a working cell with a lithium metal anode, garnet electrolyte and a high-voltage cathode by applying the newly developed interface chem.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqFRbtT_Dwn17Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liRjsYPPSdSBA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XitFamt7%252FK&amp;md5=48bd92a1395c604cb576eaf0b0c2bf0a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1039/C7SC05185A"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, J. P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Du, Y. P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Lanthanide Doping Induced Electrochemical Enhancement of Na<sub>2</sub>Ti<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub> Anodes for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Chem. Sci.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2018</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>9</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">3421</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">3425</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Lanthanide doping induced electrochemical enhancement of Na2Ti3O7 anodes for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xia, Jiale; Zhao, Hongyang; Pang, Wei Kong; Yin, Zongyou; Zhou, Bo; He, Gang; Guo, Zaiping; Du, Yaping</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Science</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">9</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">14</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3421-3425</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CSHCCN</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2041-6520</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na2Ti3O7 is considered as a promising anode material for sodium ion batteries (SIBs) due to its excellent high-rate performance compared with hard carbons.  However, the electrochem. performance of Na2Ti3O7 is heavily limited by its low elec. cond.  In this study, we synthesized a series of lanthanide (Ln = La, Ce, Nd, Sm, Gd, Er, and Yb) doped microsized Na2Ti3O7 anode materials and systematically studied the electrochem. performance.  Compared with pristine Na2Ti3O7, all the doped samples show superior electrochem. performance.  Esp., the Yb3+ doped sample not only delivers a high reversible capacity of 89.4 mA h g-1 at 30C, but also maintains 71.6 mA h g-1 at 5C after 1600 cycles, nearly twice that of pristine Na2Ti3O7.  It is found for the first time that the enhancement in doped samples is attributed to the introduction of lanthanides which induces lattice distortion and oxygen vacancies.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoYSnjk39hc1bVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liRjsYPPSdSBA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXivFGlsr0%253D&amp;md5=f03ac5096ff0e8787fd916f6ffc54a99</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1039/D1NJ00262G"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, S.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sponge-like NaFe2PO4(SO4)2@rGO as a high-performance cathode material for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Shao-Fang; Hou, Xian-Kun; Gu, Zhen-Yi; Meng, Yun-Feng; Zhao, Chen-De; Zhang, Hong-Xia; Wu, Xing-Long</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">New Journal of Chemistry</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The traditional methods of prepg. cathode materials usually require long time consuming and high temp. annealing, which consume a lot of material and financial resources.  Herein, we report an ultrafast self-propagating combustion process to prep. a sponge-like NaFe2PO4(SO4)2@reduced graphene oxide (NFPS@rGO) material with favorable surface characteristics.  This product is successfully prepd. in a short time and is well coated with reduced graphene oxide due to its loose porous surface structure.  Benefiting from the carbon coating, NFPS@rGO demonstrates an enhanced rate and cyclic performance than that of the pristine NFPS.  The discharge specific capacity of NFPS@rGO still retains 43.4 mA h g-1 at a high c.d. of 500 mA g-1.  After 100 cycles at 50 mA g-1, the capacity retention of NFPS@rGO is 83.3%, which is also higher than NFPS by 34.7%.  In addn., the fabricated hard carbon‖NFPS@rGO full-cell exhibits a favorable rate and cyclic performance.  This study provides meaningful guidance for the modification of cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries and promotes its practical possibility.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrvKmAtlx7ZZbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liRjsYPPSdSBA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXlt1akt7o%253D&amp;md5=b3c997be6a086facb42eb1919ebf44c9</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1016/j.jechem.2020.11.039"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, H.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Tempura-like carbon/carbon composite as advanced anode materials for k-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liang, Hao-Jie; Gu, Zhen-Yi; Zheng, Xue-Ying; Li, Wen-Hao; Zhu, Ling-Yun; Sun, Zhong-Hui; Meng, Yun-Feng; Yu, Hai-Yue; Hou, Xian-Kun; Wu, Xing-Long</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Energy Chemistry</span>
